The Priority and Degrees of Unity
The author defines degrees of unity and their priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four degrees of unity and their logical ordering according to priority.

Core Idea
Unity has degrees: personal unity is most deserving, followed by species, genus, and accident.

In this passage, the author establishes that the term 'one' is not used univocally but with gradations of priority. He classifies four types of unity and further subdivides personal unity. This foundational taxonomy is essential for later theological arguments about God's oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall classification.
The passage uses a compact style; focus on the hierarchy.
2
Identify the four types of 'one'
Look for the terms: الواحد بالشخص, الواحد بالنوع, الواحد بالجنس, الواحد بالعرض. Note how they are ordered by priority.
3
Understand the sub-hierarchy within 'one by person'
The passage further divides personal unity into indivisible, divisible into similar parts, and divisible into dissimilar parts. Notice the priority among these.
This shows that unity is a matter of degree even within the same class.
4
Reflect on the rejection of intensity gradation
The author notes that no one claims gradation in intensity and priority because it would be unintelligible. Think about why such a claim would be problematic.
This reinforces that the classification is about priority, not intensity.
5
Connect to theological implications
Consider how this classification helps in understanding God's oneness. Which type of unity best applies to Allah?
God is one by person, indivisible – the highest degree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحداً متفاوتاً بالأولوية، فإن الواحد بالشخص أولى بالوحدة من الواحد بالنوع وهو من الواحد بالجنس وهو من الواحد بالعرض وفي الواحد بالشخص ما لا ينقسم أصلاً أولى بالوحدة مما ينقسم إلى أجزاء متشابهة، وهو مما ينقسم إلى أجزاء متخالفة، ولم يقل أحد بالتفاوت في الأشدية والأقدمية لكونه غير معقول.
One, differing in priority. For the one by person is more deserving of unity than the one by species, which is [more deserving] than the one by genus, which is [more deserving] than the one by accident. And among the one by person, that which is indivisible at all is more deserving of unity than that which divides into similar parts, and that [which divides into similar parts] is [more deserving] than that which divides into dissimilar parts. And no one has claimed gradation in intensity and priority because it is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the degrees of unity (al-wahda). The author explains that the term 'one' (al-wahid) is used in an analogical (tashkik) sense, with different degrees of priority. The most deserving of unity is the 'one by person' (al-wahid bi al-shakhs), which refers to an individual that is indivisible. Next is the 'one by species' (al-wahid bi al-naw‘), then 'one by genus' (al-wahid bi al-jins), and finally 'one by accident' (al-wahid bi al-‘arad). Within the 'one by person', the indivisible individual has priority over one that divides into similar parts (like water), and that in turn has priority over one that divides into dissimilar parts (like a body). The author notes that no one has claimed gradation in intensity and priority (al-ashaddiyya wa al-aqdamiyya) because such a concept would be unintelligible in this context.

Key Terms
الواحد بالشخص
الواحد بالنوع
الواحد بالجنس
الواحد بالعرض
الوحدة
التشكيك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All 'ones' are equal in unity.
Correction: The author clarifies that they have gradations in priority, with the one by person being most deserving.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which type of 'one' is most deserving of unity?
A) The one by accident
B) The one by genus
C) The one by person that is indivisible
D) The one by species

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'one' is analogical, with personal unity being the highest degree and serving as the basis for understanding God's absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'God is One,' do you truly intend the highest, indivisible sense of unity, or do you sometimes unconsciously lower it to a lesser degree? What in your life reveals your actual understanding?

The passage teaches that 'unity by accident' is the least deserving. Reflect: are there aspects of your faith that you treat as accidental—mere habits or culture—rather than essential? What would it take to elevate them to the level of personal unity with truth?

Multiplicity and the Mental Nature of Numbers
The author defines multiplicity and lists its properties, proving numbers are mental constructs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that numbers are mental constructs (i‘tibāriyya) and the key properties of multiplicity, including duality, infinite increase, and the impossibility of external existence.

Core Idea
Numbers are conceptual, not external; they exist only in the mind.

This passage from the author's discussion on multiplicity lays out the foundational understanding that numbers are not real attributes in the external world but are conceptual constructions in the intellect. The author begins by defining multiplicity and its first rank, duality, then enumerates several rulings that clarify the nature of numbers. By the end, you will see why numbers like 'two' cannot exist outside the mind and how this fits into the broader kalām framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get an overall sense.
2
Identify the definition of multiplicity
Notice that the author defines multiplicity starting from duality (ithnayniyya) and explains how numbers increase by adding ones.
Duality is the first rank of multiplicity.
3
Examine the six rulings (ahkām)
The author lists several rulings indicated by the discussion. Find each one and understand what it claims about numbers.
These rulings include analogy, the first number being two, types of numbers, composition from ones, infinity, and mental existence.
4
Focus on the proof for mental existence
The last ruling provides a proof that numbers are conceptual. Pay attention to the thought experiment about a 'one' in the east and a 'one' in the west, and the argument about Zayd and 'Amr.
This is the key argument for numbers being i‘tibāriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وكذا الكثرة فإن أولى مراتبها الاثنينية ويحصل من انضمام واحد إلى واحد ثم يتزايد بزيادة الآحاد لا إلى نهاية أنواعاً مختلفة اللوازم متحصلة في العقل من اعتبار انضمام الآحاد حتى لو اعتبر واحد في المشرق مع واحد في المغرب حصلت الاثنينية من غير أن يتحقق قيام أمرهما كيف ولو تحققت لقامت بالمجموع فيلزم في كل واحد شيء منها وليس سوى الوحدة الاعتبارية يشير إلى أحكام منها أن الكثرة مقول بالتشكيك لكونها في كل عدد أشد منها فيما دونه ومنها أن أول مراتب العدد الاثنينية بمعنى أن الاثنين عدد والواحد ليس بعدد لصدق الحد وهو الكم المنفصل عليهما دونه وما قيل إذ الفرد الأول أعني الواحد ليس بعدد فكذا الزوج الأول ليس بشيء ومنها أن الأعداد أنواع مختلفة لاختلاف لوازمها من الزوجية والفردية والاسمية والمنطقية ومنها أنها متألفة من الآحاد فأجزاء العشرة واحد عشر مرات لا خمسة وخمسة أو ستة وأربعة أو سبعة وثلاثة إذ لا رجحان لشيء من ذلك بخلاف الواحد فإنه يترجح بأنه لا أقل منه وأن الاثنين إنما يتألف منه ولأن مجرد زيادة الواحد يوجب حصول نوع آخر من العدد ومنها أنها غير متناهية لأن كل عدد فرض فإنه يمكن زيادة الواحد عليه ومنها أنها أمور اعتبارية متحصلة في العقل دون الخارج لأنا إذا اعتبرنا انضمام واحد في المشرق إلى واحد في المغرب حكم العقل بحصول الاثنينية لهما من غير أن يحصل لهما أمر بحسب الخارج ولأن أجزاءها أمور اعتبارية هي الواحدات ولأنها لو تحققت في الخارج فإذا قلنا لزيد وعمر مثلاً هما اثنان فقيام الاثنينية إما بأحدهما أو بكل منهما وهو ظاهر الاستحالة أو بمجموعهما فيلزم في كل منهما شيء منها وليس سوى الوحدة الاعتبارية.
He said: And likewise multiplicity, for the foremost of its ranks is duality, and it results from the joining of one to one, then it increases by the increase of ones without end, [into] different types with differing concomitants, obtained in the intellect from considering the joining of ones, such that if one considers a one in the east along with a one in the west, duality results without the existence of something subsisting by them being actualized. How [could it be actualized]? And if it were actualized, it would subsist by the aggregate, and then each one would have a part of it, and there is nothing but the conceptual unity. He indicates rulings: among them that multiplicity is said by analogy, because in every number it is more intense than in what is below it; and among them that the first rank of number is duality, meaning that two is a number and one is not a number because the definition – which is discrete quantity – applies to them, not to it; and what is said: since the first odd, namely one, is not a number, so likewise the first even is not a thing; and among them that numbers are different types due to the difference of their concomitants from evenness, oddness, primeness, and composite-ness; and among them that they are composed of ones, so the parts of ten are one ten times, not five and five, or six and four, or seven and three, because there is no preference for any of that, unlike the one, for it is preferred by being such that there is nothing less than it, and that two is only composed from it, and because the mere addition of one necessitates the occurrence of another type of number; and among them that they are infinite, because for every number assumed, it is possible to add one to it; and among them that they are conceptual things obtained in the intellect, not externally, because if we consider the joining of a one in the east to a one in the west, the intellect judges the occurrence of duality for them without an external thing occurring for them, and because their parts are conceptual things, namely the ones, and because if they were actualized externally, then if we say for Zayd and 'Amr, for example, they are two, the subsistence of duality either belongs to one of them, or to each of them, which is clearly impossible, or to both of them together, and then each of them would have a part of it, and there is nothing but the conceptual unity.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a definition and exposition of multiplicity (al-kathra) and its properties, particularly the nature of numbers as mental constructs (umūr i‘tibāriyya). The author begins by stating that multiplicity is like unity in being analogical, and its foremost rank is duality (al-ithnayniyya), which arises from the conjunction of one to one. Numbers then increase by adding ones infinitely, producing different types with different properties. A key argument for numbers being mental rather than external is the thought experiment of considering a 'one' in the east and a 'one' in the west: the intellect still judges them as two without any external attribute. The author then enumerates several rulings (ahkām) indicated by this discussion: (1) Multiplicity is said by analogy (tashkīk), i.e., it applies to numbers in degrees of intensity. (2) The first number is two, because one does not satisfy the definition of number as discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfaṣil). (3) Numbers are different types due to properties like evenness, oddness, prime (ismiyya? likely 'prime' or 'composite'), and composite (manṭiqiyya? actually mantiqiyya may be a term for composite numbers). (4) Numbers are composed of ones; for example, ten is composed of one repeated ten times, not of other combinations, because there is no reason to prefer any other combination. (5) Numbers are infinite because one can always add one. (6) Numbers are conceptual (i‘tibāriyya) and exist only in the intellect, not outside. The author provides a proof: if we consider two individuals like Zayd and 'Amr, the concept of 'two' cannot subsist in one alone, nor in each separately, nor in the aggregate without each having a part of it, which is impossible; therefore it is a conceptual unity. The teacher should note that this argument establishes numbers as mental constructs (i‘tibārāt) in the Ash‘arī framework.

Key Terms
الكثرة
الاثنينية
العدد
الكم المنفصل
الزوجية
الفردية
الأمور الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Numbers exist externally as real attributes.
Correction: They are purely mental constructs (umūr i‘tibāriyya) as proven by the thought experiment and impossibility of external subsistence.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the rulings (ahkām) mentioned in this passage about multiplicity?
A) Multiplicity is said by analogy.
B) The first number is two, not one.
C) Numbers are infinite.
D) Numbers exist externally as real attributes.

Key Takeaway
Numbers are conceptual constructs that exist only in the intellect, not as real attributes in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you measuring your deeds with mental concepts while neglecting their true quality? Commit to evaluating your actions by their essence, not their count.
?

How can you free yourself from the illusion of numerical superiority and instead focus on the sincerity and reality of your own heart? Make a firm intention to avoid envy by remembering that numbers are mental constructs.
?

In what areas of your life do you artificially unite things—like your past mistakes with your present identity—creating a false sense of continuity? How can you break this mental habit to see each moment as a fresh opportunity before Allah? Resolve to not let the sum of your past define your current state.

6 min
Numbers as Conceptual Entities in the Mind
Al-Taftazani argues that numbers are infinite and purely conceptual, using the example of twoness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why numbers are considered conceptual entities (iʿtibāri) in the mind rather than external realities, and how the arguments from infinity and the impossibility of external twoness support this view.

Core Idea
Numbers are mental constructs, not external properties.

This passage from al-Taftazani presents a classic kalam argument for the conceptual nature of numbers. It uses two key points: the infinity of numbers and the impossibility of numbers being externally realized, illustrated by the example of twoness. By examining these arguments, we see why numbers are considered purely mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: two main claims followed by supporting evidence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage states two points about numbers: they are infinite, and they are conceptual (iʿtibāri) realized in the mind. Underline each claim.
3
Understand the trilemma against external twoness
Focus on the final proof: if twoness were external, it would have to inhere in one, in each, or in the aggregate. Each option leads to impossibility. This forces the conclusion that twoness is a mental concept.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters for kalam: numbers are used in proofs about God's unity and attributes. Understanding their conceptual nature avoids attributing multiplicity to God.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنها غير متناهية، لأن كل عدد فرض، فإنه يمكن زيادة الواحد عليه، ومنها أنها أمور اعتبارية متحصلة في العقل دون الخارج، لأنا إذا اعتبرنا انضمام واحد في المشرق إلى واحد في المغرب حكم العقل بحصول الاثنينية لهما من غير أن يحصل لهما أمر بحسب الخارج ولأن أجزاءها أمور اعتبارية هي الواحدات، ولأنها لو تحققت في الخارج. فإذا قلنا لزيد وعمر و مثلاً هما اثنان، فقيام الاثنينية، إما بأحدهما، أو بكل منهما، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة، أو بمجموعهما، فيلزم في كل منهما شيء منها، وليس سوى الوحدة الاعتبارية.
And among them is that they are infinite, because for every number assumed, it is possible to add one to it. And among them is that they are conceptual affairs (umūr iʿtibāriyya) realized in the mind, not in the external [world], because when we consider the joining of one in the east to one in the west, the mind judges the occurrence of twoness for them without any matter occurring to them externally; and because their parts are conceptual affairs, namely the units; and because if they were realized externally, then when we say, for example, 'Zayd and ʿAmr are two', the subsistence of twoness would be either in one of them, or in each of them – which is evidently impossible – or in their aggregate, which would require in each of them something of it, and there is nothing besides the conceptual unit.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the conceptual nature of numbers. The author states two points: first, numbers are infinite because you can always add one to any assumed number; second, numbers are conceptual affairs (umūr iʿtibāriyya) realized in the mind, not in the external world. The proof for the second point: when we mentally join two physically separate units (e.g., one in the east and one in the west), the mind judges twoness for them even though nothing new occurs externally. Furthermore, the parts of numbers are units, which are conceptual. If numbers were external, then when we say 'Zayd and ʿAmr are two', the twoness would have to inhere either in one individual (impossible because then only one would have twoness), or in each (impossible because then each would be two entirely), or in their aggregate (impossible because each would still only have a part of the twoness). Since all options are impossible, twoness cannot be external; it is only the conceptual unit that exists.

Key Terms

العدد
الاثنينية
الوحدة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think numbers are real properties in the external world, like color or weight.
Correction: This passage shows that numbers are purely mental constructs. They do not exist independently in external objects; they arise from the mind's act of considering units.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that twoness cannot be an external reality?
A) Because numbers are finite
B) Because if twoness were external, it would have to inhere in one of the two individuals, in each, or in their aggregate, all of which are impossible
C) Because twoness is a divine attribute
D) Because units are not abstract

Key Takeaway
Numbers are not external realities but mental constructs; the impossibility of locating twoness externally proves its conceptual nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of numbers as existing 'out there' – in your bank account, your age, your score. But this lesson says numbers are mental constructs. If that is true, why do you treat them as if they are more real than your intentions or your relationship with Allah?
?
This passage argues that 'twoness' cannot be located anywhere outside the mind. Consider your own sense of being 'one' or 'two' with someone. When you say 'we are two', where does that 'twoness' truly reside? What does that imply about the reality of your connections?
?
If numbers are purely conceptual, then everything you count – your years, your deeds, your possessions – are ultimately mental labels. Does that make them less real or more urgent? How does this change how you view the accounting on the Day of Judgment?

The Impossibility of Two Becoming One: Initial Proofs
Al-Taftazani states the impossibility of union and gives two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments Al-Taftazani uses to prove that two distinct entities cannot become one.

Core Idea
Intrinsic identity differences prevent two things from merging into one.

Before diving into objections and replies, Al-Taftazani lays out the core reasons why union of two distinct things is impossible. These proofs rely on the nature of identity and the logical consequences of staying distinct after union. Understanding these foundational arguments is crucial for following the dialectic that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by two distinct proofs.
The proofs are linked by 'because' (لأن).
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the first proof based on intrinsic difference of quiddities or identities. Find the second proof based on what happens if they remain or not after union.
The second proof has a disjunction: either they remain or they don't.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why does the author consider these proofs sufficient? What assumption underlies the idea that difference of essences is permanent?
This is a key a priori argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث يمتنع اتحاد الاثنين . لأن اختلاف الماهيتين . أو لمجرد الهويتين ذاتي لا يزول، ولأنهما إن بقيا كانا اثنين وإلا فكان فناء لأحدهما، وبقاء للآخر، أو فناء لهما وحدوث ثالث . ورد الأول بأنه ليس أوضح من الدعوى - والثاني بمنع الاثنينية على تقدير البقاء، وإنما يلزم لو لم يتحدا، فغير إلى أنهما إن كانا موجودين كانا اثنين وإلا فكما مر ورد بجواز أن يكونا موجودين بوجود واحد، فدفع بأنه إما أحد الوجودين، أو ثالث فأجيب بأنه نفس الوجودين صارا واحداً. فادعى أن الحكم ضروري والمذكور (تنبيه) . بأن يكون هناك شيئان فيصيرا شيئاً واحداً لا بطريق الوحدة الاتصالية، كما إذا جمع الماء ان في إناء واحد . أو الاجتماعية ، كما إذا امتزج الماء والتراب فصار طيناً، أو
The third investigation: The union of two [things] is impossible. Because the difference of the quiddities (māhiyyāt) or merely of the identities (huwiyyāt) is intrinsic (dhātī) and does not disappear; and because if they remain, they would be two, otherwise it would be the annihilation of one and the survival of the other, or the annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third. The first [argument] is rebutted by saying that it is not clearer than the claim; the second by denying twoness on the assumption of survival, and it would only follow if they did not unite. Then it was changed to: if they exist, they are two; otherwise as before. It was rebutted by the permissibility that they exist with one existence. It was refuted by saying that that existence is either one of the two existences or a third. It was answered that it is the same two existences become one. So he claimed that the judgment is necessary and the mentioned (warning). That there are two things that become one thing not by way of conjunctive unity (waḥda ittiṣāliyya), as when water is gathered in one vessel, or by social unity (ijtimāʿiyya), as when water and earth mix and become clay, or
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (objection and answer) proving that two distinct entities cannot become one unified thing. The author begins by stating the impossibility of the union of two. Two proofs are given: (1) The difference in their quiddities or even just their identities is intrinsic and cannot vanish. (2) If they remain after union, they are still two; if they do not remain, then either one is annihilated and the other survives (so not union), or both are annihilated and a third arises (so not identity of the original two). Then the author presents and rebuts objections. Objection 1: The first proof is not clearer than the claim itself. Objection 2: The second proof is denied because twoness does not necessarily apply if they survive after union; it only would if they were separate. The author then revises the argument: if they exist (after union), they are two; otherwise not. The opponent rebuts that they could exist with a single existence. The author refutes by saying that single existence would be either one of the original two or a third, not both. The opponent answers that the two existences themselves become one. The author then claims the judgment is necessary. The passage ends with a 'warning' (tanbīh) that begins to describe ways two things might become one that are not true union: conjunctive (like water in a vessel), social (like mixing water and earth into clay), and possibly other types (the text breaks off).

Key Terms
اتحاد
الماهية
الهوية
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Al-Taftazani's first proof rely on?
A) The impossibility of annihilation
B) The intrinsic difference of quiddities or identities
C) The possibility of a third entity
D) The necessity of twoness after union

Key Takeaway
Two distinct things cannot become one because their essential differences are permanent and any outcome after attempted union fails to produce true identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a logical proof that two distinct things cannot truly become one. Where in your own life do you try to merge two incompatible things—like worldly ambitions and spiritual sincerity? How does this proof expose the impossibility of that union?
?
This passage argues that intrinsic differences cannot be erased. Think of a relationship where you expect someone to change their essential nature to fit your desires. How does this lesson call you to accept differences rather than demand impossible unity?
?
The second proof shows that any attempt at union either fails (remains two) or destroys one or both. Have you ever sacrificed part of your identity to 'unite' with a group or person? How does this lesson challenge you to preserve your God-given distinctness?

Responding to Objections on the Union of Two
Dialectical defense of the impossibility of union and introduction of apparent unions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani defends his proof against objections and introduces apparent unions that are not true identity change.

Core Idea
Objections to the impossibility of union are met with logical distinctions, and apparent unions are merely conjunctive or social.

After presenting his proofs, Al-Taftazani anticipates and responds to several objections. The dialectic sharpens the argument. He then clarifies that what people call 'union' is often just conjunction or mixture, not true identity change. This lesson traces the twists of the debate and the clarification of what union is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchanges
Follow the sequence: objection, rebuttal, revised argument, counter-rebuttal, final answer. Notice how each side refines the claim.
The structure is typical of kalam debates.
2
Identify the key moves
Find the point where the opponent introduces 'one existence', and see how Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing that existence from the two originals.
This is the core of the logical defense.
3
Understand the 'warning' (tanbih)
Read the beginning of the tanbih. Note the two types of apparent union: conjunctive and social. Why are these not true unions?
The passage cuts off, but the idea is clear: these are merely combined, not one in identity.

The Impossibility of Two Becoming One
Al-Taftazani states the impossibility of two becoming one and gives two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two distinct things cannot become one according to kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Two distinct things cannot become one because their difference is essential and union leads to annihilation.

Al-Taftazani begins the third investigation by addressing a key philosophical question: can two distinct things become one? This question has implications for understanding divine attributes and the nature of change. In this passage, he provides two clear arguments demonstrating the impossibility of such union.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: claim followed by two reasons introduced by 'because'.
2
Identify the claim and the two reasons
The claim is that the union of two things is impossible. The first reason involves essential difference; the second reason considers the survival or annihilation of the two things.
3
Understand the first reason: essential difference
The difference between two essences or two individualities is essential (dhātī), meaning it cannot be removed without changing the thing itself. If it could be removed, the thing would no longer be what it is.
The term 'essence' (māhiyya) and 'individuality' (huwiyya) are key technical terms.
4
Understand the second reason: annihilation or survival
If after the supposed union both things remain, they are still two, not one. If they do not both remain, then either one is annihilated (then it's not union but replacement) or both are annihilated and a third thing comes into being (then it's not the original two becoming one).
This is a logical disjunction covering all possibilities.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument matters in kalam. It is used to discuss the unity of God and the impossibility of attributing composition to the Divine Essence.
This passage is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث يمتنع اتحاد الاثنين (١) . لأن اختلاف الماهيتين (٢) . أو لمجرد (۳) الهويتين (٤) ذاتي لا يزول (ه)، ولأنهما إن بقيا ) كانا اثنين وإلا كان فناء لأحدهما، وبقاء للآخر، أو فناء لهما وحدوث ثالث .
The third investigation: The union of two things is impossible. Because the difference of the two essences, or merely the two individualities, is essential and not removable, and because if they remain they would be two, and otherwise there would be annihilation of one and survival of the other, or annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (the third investigation) in which the author states a philosophical claim: two distinct things cannot become one. He gives two reasons. First, the difference between two essences or two individuals is essential (dhātī), meaning it inheres in their very nature and cannot be removed. If it were removed, the thing would no longer be itself. Second, if the two things were to survive after the supposed union, they would still be two, not one. If they did not both survive, then either one is annihilated while the other remains (so there is no union of two, only replacement), or both are annihilated and something entirely new comes into being (which is not the same as the original two becoming one). Thus, the claim that two become one is impossible.

Key Terms
اتحاد الاثنين
اختلاف الماهيتين
الهويتين
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument denies any kind of combination or mixture.
Correction: It does not deny that two things can combine to form a mixture; it denies that each becomes the other while remaining itself.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given for the impossibility of two becoming one?
A) Because difference is essential
B) Because if they remain they are two, otherwise one is annihilated or both are annihilated
C) Because union is impossible
D) Because they are different

Key Takeaway
Two distinct things cannot become one because their essential difference is inextricable and any apparent union results in either two things or annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Consider a relationship where you have tried to merge your identity with someone else's. How does the principle that 'two cannot become one' challenge your understanding of unity and partnership?

Clarifying the Type of Union in Kalam Debate
Al-Taftazani clarifies that the union in question is not through connection, mixture, or change, but one becoming the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific meaning of 'union' (ittiḥād) that al-Taftazani is discussing and how it differs from ordinary forms of combination.

Core Idea
The union under debate is one thing becoming another exactly, not mixing or transforming.

Al-Taftazani begins with a clarifying remark (tanbīh) to prevent misunderstanding. He lists four common types of combination that are not the subject of the theological debate. This careful distinction is necessary because the concept of 'union' has multiple meanings, and the debate concerns a very specific logical sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's exact wording.
Notice the technical terms: 'waḥda ittiṣāliyya', 'ijtimāʿiyya', etc.
2
Identify the four excluded types of unity
The author lists four types of combination that are NOT the intended meaning. Find each one and note how they differ from the intended union.
The examples help: water+water, water+soil, water+air, color change.
3
Understand the intended union
The intended union is 'one becomes the other, the very same other'. Reflect on why this is distinct from mixing, transformation, or composition.
This sets the stage for the proof of impossibility in the next section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمذكور (تنبيه) بأن يكون هناك شيئان فيصيرا شيئاً واحداً لا بطريق الوحدة الاتصالية، كما إذا جمع الماء ان في إناء واحد . أو الاجتماعية ، كما إذا امتزج الماء والتراب فصار طيناً، أو الكون والفساد كالماء والهواء صارا بالغليان هواء واحداً ، أو الاستحالة كلون الجسم كان سواداً وبياضاً ، فصار سواداً بل بأن يصير أحدهما الآخر الصائر بعينه إياه وذلك لوجهين :
And what is mentioned is a remark: that there are two things that become one thing, not by way of connecting unity, like when two waters are collected in one vessel; nor by social unity, like when water and soil mix to become mud; nor by generation and corruption, like water and air becoming one air through boiling; nor by transformation, like the color of a body that was black and white becoming black. Rather, it is that one becomes the other, the very same other. And that is through two aspects:
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a clarifying remark (tanbīh) by the author. He defines the specific type of union being discussed: it is not ordinary composition or mixture. He lists four types of combination that are not what he means: (1) connecting unity (al-waḥda al-ittiṣāliyya) like two masses of water in one container; (2) social unity (al-ijtimāʿiyya) like water and earth mixing to form mud; (3) generation and corruption (al-kawn wa al-fasād) like water and air turning into air through boiling; (4) transformation (al-istiḥāla) like a body's color changing from black and white to black. All these involve one thing becoming something else or combination. The union under debate is stronger: it is that one thing becomes the other, the very same individual, without any change or addition. He then says that there are two reasons (wajhayn) for its impossibility, which he proceeds to detail in the following lessons.

Key Terms
تنبيه
وحدة اتصالية
اجتماعية
كون وفساد
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying all forms of combination or union.
Correction: He is only excluding common physical or qualitative unions, and focusing on a precise logical sense: one thing becoming another identical.

Checkpoint
Which type of union is NOT included in the author's intended meaning?
A) One thing becoming another exactly
B) Mixing of water and soil
C) Two things becoming one without change
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
This passage defines the specific type of union (ittiḥād) under discussion as one thing becoming another exactly, distinct from physical mixing, transformation, or composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own faith, have you ever imagined that God's oneness (tawḥīd) is like a mixture of His attributes with His essence, as if they were separate components? How does this lesson's clear rejection of such unions expose a hidden shirk in your thinking? What will you do to purge that from your heart today?
?
You seek to become closer to the Prophet ﷺ in character. But do you try to merely add good qualities to yourself (like a transformation) or do you realize you must become like him exactly, without mixing in your own desires? Which of the four rejected unions describes your current approach, and how will you change?
?
This passage defines union as one thing becoming another without any change. How does this challenge your understanding of God's relationship with His creation? Can you honestly say you have never thought of God as 'mixing' with the world in some way? Repent of that notion now and affirm His absolute transcendence.

8 min
The Impossibility of Two Beings Sharing One Existence
The author refutes the possibility of two beings united by a single existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical difficulties in claiming that two beings could be united by a single existence, and why the appeal to necessity is problematic.

Core Idea
Two things cannot become one without contradiction.

This passage presents a dialectical argument in which the author rebuts an opponent's claim that two beings could share a single existence. The argument reveals why such a union leads to logical absurdities, and why the opponent's reliance on 'necessity' is not self-evident. By following the back-and-forth, you will see how kalam thinkers rigorously test metaphysical claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the structure: the author presents a rebuttal, then an answer, then a criticism.
Pay attention to the conditional reasoning: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the logical alternatives
The passage considers two possibilities for two beings existing with a single existence: either they have two existences (making them two) or one existence. Then it analyzes the one-existence case into subcases.
List the subcases: the one existence is either one of the original two, or a third.
3
Understand the opponent's answer
The opponent claims that the two beings exist with a single existence that is identical to the two original existences, so they become one. The author questions whether this is coherent.
This is the key move that leads to the criticism.
4
Analyze the author's criticism
The author points out that the only escape is to claim that the impossibility of union is a necessary judgment. But he questions whether that necessity is obvious, and argues that the impossibility of two existences uniting is not clearer than the impossibility of two things uniting in general.
This exposes a weakness in the opponent's appeal to intuition.
5
Reflect on the dialectical style
Notice how the argument proceeds through objection, rebuttal, answer, and criticism. This is typical of kalam reasoning. The author does not settle the issue but leaves the reader to judge.
Ask yourself: Is the impossibility of two becoming one truly necessary?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك (۱) لو لم يكونا موجودين لوجود واحد، فدفع هذا الاعتراض، بأنها لو كانا موجودين فإما بوجودين فيكونان اثنين لا واحداً وإما بوجود واحد، فذلك إما أحد الوجودين الأولين، فيكون فناء لأحدهما ، وبقاء للآخر، أو غيرهما فيكون فناء لهما وحدوث ثالث .
هذا فأجيب عن الدفع بأنهما موجودان بوجود واحد هو نفس الوجودين الأولين صارا واحداً، فلم يمكن التقصي عن هذا المنع إلا بأن الحكم بامتناع اتحاد الاثنين ضروري.
والمذكور في معرض الاستدلال تنبيه بزيادة بيان وتفصيل، وأنت خبير بحال دعوى الضرورة في محل النزاع، وبأن امتناع اتحاد الوجودين ليس بأوضح من امتناع اتحاد الاثنين على الإطلاق.
(۱) سقط من (أ) لفظ ذلك .
That (1): if they were not existing for a single existence, then he rebuts this objection by saying: if they were existing, then either with two existences so they would be two not one, or with one existence, then that existence is either one of the two original existences, so it would be the annihilation of one and survival of the other, or other than them, so it would be the annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third. Then it was answered to the rebuttal that they exist with a single existence which is identical to the two original existences, so they became one. So it was not possible to extricate oneself from this prevention except by [saying] that the judgment of the impossibility of the union of two is necessary. And what is mentioned in the context of the argument is a reminder with increased clarification and detail, and you are aware of the status of the claim of necessity in the locus of dispute, and that the impossibility of the union of two existences is not clearer than the impossibility of the union of two absolutely. (1) The word 'that' is missing from copy (أ).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument and counter-argument. The author begins by describing a rebuttal to an objection: if two beings were to exist with a single existence, then either they exist with two distinct existences (making them two, not one) or with one existence. If with one existence, that existence is either one of the original two (so one perishes, the other remains) or a third existence (so both original existences perish and a new one arises). The opponent then answers that the two beings exist with a single existence that is identical to the two original existences, so they become one. The author criticizes this answer, stating that the only way to avoid this problem is to claim that the impossibility of two becoming one is a necessary judgment. But the author questions this claim of necessity, noting that the impossibility of two existences uniting is not more obvious than the impossibility of any two things uniting. Thus, the argument is left unresolved, exposing a weakness in the opponent's position. The passage concludes with a reminder that this is just a clarification, and the reader should judge the strength of the necessity claim.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two beings can share a single existence without logical contradiction.
Correction: The passage shows that any attempt to explain such union leads to absurdities (annihilation of one or both beings, or the creation of a third). The only escape is an appeal to necessity, which the author questions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if two beings exist with a single existence that is a third existence (other than the two original existences)?
A) One being perishes and the other remains.
B) Both beings perish and a third being arises.
C) The two beings become one without any loss.
D) The two beings remain two with one existence.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two beings uniting in one existence is not self-evidently necessary; the argument reveals logical absurdities that make such union untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your heart: are there hidden dependencies or 'partners' you rely on as if they were co-existent with Allah? How can you let this rational clarity deepen your conviction?
?
Consider a close human relationship where two people feel 'one'—like a spouse or dear friend. In light of this passage, what kind of unity is possible between creatures? Does your longing for intimacy ever blur the line between the oneness of God and the union of creation? How can you love others without compromising His exclusive existence?
?
The author admits that the impossibility of two becoming one is not self-evident to all. Are there core beliefs of your faith that you hold without examining their rational grounds? Will you commit today to studying the proofs behind your creed, so your faith is not just inherited but intellectually owned?

Al-Taghayur: Definition and Conditions of Otherness
The author defines al-taghayur as a property of multiplicity and specifies its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-taghayur (difference) as a property of multiplicity and the conditions that must be met for two things to be considered 'other' (ghayr).

Core Idea
Two things are 'other' only if both exist and can be separated—even if only conceptually.

This passage from al-Maqasid defines a key term in Islamic theology: al-taghayur (difference). It is a property of multiplicity, meaning it only applies when there are multiple existents. The author specifies the precise conditions for calling two things 'other', and clarifies exceptions like parts and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own phrasing shape your understanding before analyzing.
Notice the two conditions for 'otherness' in the first sentence.
2
Identify the two conditions for otherness
The passage states that two things are 'other' only if they are both existent and their separation is permissible. Identify these two conditions.
Separation can be sensory or conceptual—both are valid.
3
Understand the exceptions
The part-whole relationship and attribute-described relationship are excluded from otherness because they cannot be separated. Ask yourself why these are inseparable.
The examples clarify that even conceptual separation is sufficient, but these pairs lack that.
4
Reflect on the examples and footnotes
Consider the idioms like 'other than Zayd' and 'other than ten'. Then read the footnotes which add that difference entails multiplicity and excludes nonexistents.
This shows that otherness is an existential concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
التغاير من خواص الكثرة
(قال) المبحث الرابع من خواص الكثرة التغاير (۱) والغيران عند مشايخنا موجودان (۲) جاز انفكاكهما فالجزء مع الكل (۳) لا هو ولا غيره، وكذلك الموصوف والصفة، ولذا يصح ما في الدار غير زيد، وليس في يدي غير عشرة، مع أن فيهما الأجزاء والصفات ..
(۱) التغاير يختص بموصوف الكثرة فيكون التغاير مستلزماً للكثرة فكلما تحقق الاتصاف في التغاير فقد تحقق في ذلك المحل الاتصاف بالكثرة إذ المختص بالشيء لا يتحقق في غيره .
(۲) فيخرج من هذا التعريف المعدومان والموجود والمعدوم فلا يوصفان بالغيرية وهذا بناء على أن التغاير من الموجوديات كالاختلاف والتضاد فلا يتصف بها المعدوم .
(۳) سواء كان حسياً كاليد من زيد والسقف من البيت أو عقلياً كالحيوانية من الإنسانية.
The fourth topic: Difference (al-taghayur) is a property of multiplicity (al-kathra). He said: The fourth topic: from the properties of multiplicity is difference. The two others (al-ghirani) according to our teachers are two existents whose separation is permissible. Thus the part with the whole is neither it nor other than it, and likewise the attribute and the described. Hence it is correct to say 'what is in the house is other than Zayd' and 'I have nothing other than ten' even though they contain parts and attributes. (1) Difference is specific to something characterized by multiplicity, so difference entails multiplicity. Whenever the description of difference is realized, the description of multiplicity is realized in that locus, because what is specific to a thing is not realized in other than it. (2) Thus from this definition are excluded the two nonexistents and the existent and the nonexistent, so they are not described as otherness. This is based on the fact that difference is among existential matters like disagreement and opposition, so the nonexistent is not described by them. (3) Whether it is sensory like the hand from Zayd and the ceiling from the house, or conceptual like animality from humanity.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines al-taghayur as a property of multiplicity. It states that two things are considered 'other' (ghayr) only if they are both existent and it is possible to separate them. The author then clarifies exceptions: the part and the whole are not considered 'other' to each other because the part cannot exist independently of the whole; similarly, an attribute and its described object are not 'other' because the attribute is inseparable. Practical examples show that idioms like 'other than Zayd' are valid even when the objects contain inseparable parts or attributes, because the separation is conceptual. The footnotes add: difference is specific to what has multiplicity; the definition excludes nonexistents and pairs of existent and nonexistent; and separation can be sensory (like a hand and a body) or conceptual (like animality and humanity).

Key Terms
التغاير
الكثرة
الغيران
الانفكاك
موجودان
معدومان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that difference requires actual physical separation.
Correction: The passage shows that conceptual separation (such as between animality and humanity) is sufficient for two things to be considered 'other'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must be met for two things to be considered 'other' (ghayr)?
A) They must be physically separate.
B) They must both exist and permit separation.
C) They must be neither parts nor attributes.
D) They must be conceptual entities.

Key Takeaway
Al-taghayur requires two existents that can be separated, whether physically or conceptually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that a part is not 'other' than the whole. In your own life, do you sometimes see yourself as an isolated part of your family or community, acting as if you can be separated from them? What would change if you lived knowing you are not other than them?
?
The passage says an attribute is not 'other' than its subject. Think of a trait you dislike about yourself—anger, impatience, laziness. Do you treat it as if it is separate from you, something you can simply discard without changing your core? How does the truth that it is not other than you challenge your approach to self-improvement?

Conceptual Separation and Difference in Kalam
The author answers the objection that difference requires two-sided separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conceptual separation suffices for difference in kalam, and how objections about the Creator-world relationship and relatives are resolved.

Core Idea
Difference in kalam requires conceptual, not actual, separation.

In this passage, the author defends the definition of difference against an objection that would either make too many things different or absurdly deny difference between Creator and world. The key is that separation is understood conceptually, not existentially. A further objection about cause-effect relations is also answered, and a final note clarifies the status of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the two horns of the dilemma: one-sided separation makes part-whole and attribute-subject distinct; two-sided separation would deny Creator-world difference.
2
Grasp the answer: conceptual separation
The author clarifies that separation means the ability to conceive one without the other, even if they coexist.
3
Consider the sub-objection about relatives
The mudafan objection is answered by denying their existence as separate entities.
4
Reflect on the footnote about attributes
Attributes are neither identical to nor distinct from the described; they are inseparable.
This reinforces the conceptual separation theme.
5
Connect to the main definition of difference
This passage reinforces that difference is defined by conceptual separation, not existential independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : أن أريد الانفكاك من جانب فقط، يوجد الجزء بدون الكل والموصوف بدون الصفة أو من الجانبين. ورد الصانع مع العالم .
أجيب بأن المراد بالانفكاك من الجانبين تعقلاً. ولذا قيل هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما، ويجهل الآخر، ولا يمتنع تعقل العالم بدون الصانع، وأما من حيث إنه معلول من المضاف فقد أورد عليهم المضافان . فأجيب بأنهما غير موجودين .
(٤) فلا يقال في الصفة أنها عين الموصوف ولا أنها غيره إذ لا تنفك الصفة عن الموصوف .
If you say: if separation from one side only is intended, then the part exists without the whole and the attribute without the described, or from both sides – then the Creator with the world is problematic. It is answered that what is meant by separation from both sides is conceptual. Hence it is said: they are two such that it is valid to know one and be ignorant of the other, and it is not impossible to conceive the world without the Creator. But insofar as it is an effect of the cause, the two relatives (al-mudafan) were objected against them. So it was answered that they are nonexistent. (4) Thus it is not said of the attribute that it is identical to the described nor that it is other than it, since the attribute is inseparable from the described.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage handles an objection to the definition of difference. The objector points out that if separation from only one side is required, then a part can exist without the whole and an attribute without its subject, but if separation from both sides is required, then the Creator and the world cannot be considered different because the world depends on the Creator. The answer is that the required separation is conceptual, not actual: two things are different if we can know one while being ignorant of the other, and it is possible to conceive the world without the Creator in a conceptual sense. The author then addresses a further objection about 'two relatives' (mudafan, like cause and effect), stating that they are nonexistent in this context. The footnote clarifies that attributes are neither identical to nor other than their subject because they are inseparable.

Key Terms
الانفكاك
تعقلاً
المضافان
الصانع
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Difference requires actual independence between two things.
Correction: The passage shows that conceptual distinction – being able to conceive one without the other – is sufficient.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about the Creator and the world being considered different?
A) By denying that they are different.
B) By saying separation is conceptual, not actual.
C) By arguing the world does not depend on the Creator.
D) By claiming the Creator is part of the world.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, two things are considered different if one can be conceived without the other, even if they are always found together in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your relationship with Allah, do you treat His existence as conceptually separable from yours? Not in reality, but in your mind? Do you ever act as if the world could exist without Him, even for a moment? How does this lesson challenge the way you think about your dependence on Him?
?
If you can conceive of yourself without your attributes, like intelligence or kindness, does that mean those attributes are not truly you? How does this affect your understanding of who you are as a person and your accountability before Allah?
?
Have you ever held onto a wrong belief simply because it seemed 'obvious'? This lesson shows that what seems obvious (like actual independence for difference) can be flawed. Are you willing to let go of your comfortable assumptions when evidence demands it, even if it costs you something?

Definition of التغاير and Condition for غيران
Definition of التغاير and condition for being غيران with objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that التغاير (distinctness) is a property of multiplicity and that two things are considered غيران only if both exist and their separation is possible from both sides conceptually.

Core Idea
Distinctness requires existential separability — two things must be separately conceivable.

The author defines التغاير as a property of multiplicity and sets out the condition for two things to be considered distinct (غيران). He explains that mere difference is not enough; they must be existent and separable. This foundational concept helps us understand how parts, attributes, and relata are not truly distinct from their wholes, subjects, or correlates.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening sentences defining التغاير as a property of multiplicity.
Notice that التغاير is not just any difference but belongs to multiplicity.
2
Understand the condition
Identify the condition: two things must be existent and their separation possible.
The text says: والغيران عند مشايخنا موجودان جاز انفكاكهما
3
Examine the exceptions
See why part-whole and attribute-described are excluded — separation is impossible.
The part cannot exist without the whole; the attribute cannot exist without the described.
4
Address the objection
Read the objection about Creator-world and the answer involving conceptual separation.
The answer clarifies that separation is from both sides conceptually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
التغاير من خواص الكثرة
(قال) المبحث الرابع من خواص الكثرة التغاير (۱) والغيران عند مشايخنا موجودان (۲) جاز انفكاكهما فالجزء مع الكل (۳) لا هو ولا غيره، وكذلك الموصوف والصفة)، ولذا يصح ما في الدار غير زيد، وليس في يدي غير عشرة، مع أن فيهما الأجزاء والصفات ..
فإن قلت : أن أريد الانفكاك من جانب فقط، يوجد الجزء بدون الكل والموصوف بدون الصفة أو من الجانبين. ورد الصانع مع العالم .
،
أجيب بأن المراد بالانفكاك من الجانبين تعقلاً. ولذا قيل هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما، ويجهل الآخر، ولا يمتنع تعقل العالم بدون الصانع، وأما من حيث إنه معلول من المضاف فقد أورد عليهم المضافان . فأجيب بأنهما غير موجودين .
(۱) التغاير يختص بموصوف الكثرة فيكون التغاير مستلزماً للكثرة فكلما تحقق الاتصاف في التغاير فقد تحقق في ذلك المحل الاتصاف بالكثرة إذ المختص بالشيء لا يتحقق في غيره .
(۲) فيخرج من هذا التعريف المعدومان والموجود والمعدوم فلا يوصفان بالغيرية وهذا بناء على أن التغاير من الموجوديات كالاختلاف والتضاد فلا يتصف بها المعدوم .
(۳) سواء كان حسياً كاليد من زيد والسقف من البيت أو عقلياً كالحيوانية من الإنسانية. (٤) فلا يقال في الصفة أنها عين الموصوف ولا أنها غيره إذ لا تنفك الصفة عن الموصوف .
The fourth topic: التغاير (distinctness) is from the properties of multiplicity. (He said) The fourth topic: from the properties of multiplicity is التغاير (1). And الغيران (the two distinct things) according to our teachers are two existing things (2) whose separation is possible. Thus the part with the whole (3) is neither it nor other than it, and likewise the described and the attribute. Hence it is correct to say 'there is no one in the house other than Zayd' and 'there is nothing in my hand other than ten', even though they contain parts and attributes. If you say: If separation from one side only is intended, then the part exists without the whole, and the described without the attribute, or from both sides, and this would include the Creator with the world. The answer is that what is meant by separation from both sides is conceptually (تعقلاً). Hence it was said: They are two things such that it is valid to know one and be ignorant of the other, and it is not impossible to conceive the world without the Creator. As for it being a relatum (مضاف), they were objected to concerning the two relata (المضافان), and it was answered that they are non-existent. (1) التغاير is specific to a thing described by multiplicity, so التغاير necessitates multiplicity; whenever the description of التغاير is realized, the description of multiplicity is realized in that locus, since that which is specific to a thing is not realized in other than it. (2) Thus from this definition are excluded the two non-existents and the existent and the non-existent, so they are not described by غيرية (otherness), and this is based on the fact that التغاير is among existential properties like difference and opposition, so non-existents are not described by it. (3) Whether it is sensory, like the hand of Zayd and the ceiling of the house, or intellectual, like animality from humanity. (4) So it is not said of the attribute that it is identical to the described nor that it is other than it, since the attribute does not separate from the described.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines التغاير (distinctness) as a property of multiplicity and states the condition for two things to be considered 'غيران' (distinct). The author begins by establishing that التغاير is a property specific to multiplicity; wherever there is التغاير, there must be multiplicity. Then he gives the foundational rule: according to the teachers (مشايخنا), two things are considered distinct (غيران) only if they are both existent and their separation (انفكاك) is possible. Based on this rule, certain pairs are excluded: the part with the whole (e.g., hand and person) is neither identical to nor other than the whole because the part cannot exist separately from the whole in a way that would make it a distinct entity. Similarly, an attribute and its described subject are not considered distinct because they cannot be separated. The author illustrates this with linguistic examples: saying 'there is no one in the house other than Zayd' is valid even though Zayd's parts and attributes are in the house—this shows that parts and attributes are not counted as 'other' because they cannot exist independently. An objection is raised: if separation from one side only is considered sufficient (e.g., the part exists without the whole), then this would apply to the Creator and the world, implying they are distinct in an unacceptable way. The answer clarifies that what is meant by separation is from both sides, and conceptually (تعقلاً): two things are distinct if each can be known or conceived without the other. It is not impossible to conceive the world without the Creator, so that objection is invalid. The author also addresses the case of relata (المضافان), arguing that purely relational entities do not exist independently and therefore are not considered distinct. Footnotes further explain that التغاير necessitates multiplicity, only existent things can be described as distinct, and that the part-whole and attribute-described cases are excluded because separation is impossible.

Key Terms

غيران
انفكاك
تعقل
موجود
معدوم
جزء
كل
صفة
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any two different things (like part and whole) are متغايران.
Correction: The condition of existential separability from both sides conceptually is essential; part and whole are not truly distinct because the part cannot exist separately from the whole.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for two things to be considered غيران according to the teachers?
A) They must be both existent and their separation must be possible from both sides conceptually.
B) They must be different in essence regardless of existence.
C) They must be both non-existent or one existent and one non-existent.
D) They must be opposites like black and white.

Key Takeaway
التغاير is a property of multiplicity; two things are غيران only if both exist and their separation is conceptually possible from both sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that two things are distinct only if they can be conceptually separated from both sides. Think of an attribute you strongly identify with, such as your career or a role. Is that attribute separable from you? If it were removed, would you still be you? How does this understanding affect your reliance on that attribute rather than on your Creator?
?
The passage excludes the described and its attribute from being distinct because the attribute does not separate. Are there attributes in your life that you treat as separate from yourself—like anger, pride, or generosity—when in reality they are inseparable from your actions? How can you take ownership of them instead of seeing them as external?
?
Consider the objection about the Creator and the world: it is valid to conceive the world without the Creator, but does your daily life reflect that truth? In moments of forgetfulness, do you act as if the world is independent of Allah? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of His constant presence?
